</tbody></table>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine" title="2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine">2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine</a> the country had over 41 million people, and was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_European_countries_by_population" title="List of European countries by population">eighth-most populous country</a> in Europe. It is a <a href="/wiki/Urbanization_by_country" title="Urbanization by country">heavily urbanized country</a>, and its industrial regions in the east and southeast are the most densely populated—about 67% of its total population lives in urban areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[276]</a></sup> At that time Ukraine had a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population_density" title="List of countries by population density">population density</a> of 69.5 inhabitants per square kilometre (180 per square mile), and the overall <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_life_expectancy" title="List of countries by life expectancy">life expectancy in the country</a> at birth was 73 years (68 years for males and 77.8 years for females).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Dissolution of the Soviet Union">dissolution of the Soviet Union</a>, Ukraine's population hit a peak of roughly 52 million in 1993. However, due to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_rate" title="Death rate">death rate</a> exceeding its <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rate</a>, mass emigration, poor living conditions, and low-quality health care,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nw-20260217_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-nw-20260217-283">[278]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ukrstat-population_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-ukrstat-population-284">[279]</a></sup> the total population decreased by 6.6 million, or 12.8% from the same year to 2014.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Census_(2001)" title="Ukrainian Census (2001)">2001 census</a>, ethnic <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a> made up roughly 78% of the population, while <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> were the largest minority, at some 17.3% of the population. Small minority populations included: <a href="/wiki/Belarusians" title="Belarusians">Belarusians</a> (0.6%), <a href="/wiki/Moldovans" title="Moldovans">Moldovans</a> (0.5%), <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Tatars" title="Crimean Tatars">Crimean Tatars</a> (0.5%), <a href="/wiki/Bulgarians" title="Bulgarians">Bulgarians</a> (0.4%), <a href="/wiki/Hungarians" title="Hungarians">Hungarians</a> (0.3%), <a href="/wiki/Romanians" title="Romanians">Romanians</a> (0.3%), <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a> (0.3%), <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> (0.3%), <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> (0.2%), <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> (0.2%) and <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a> (0.2%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnic_composition_of_the_population_of_Ukraine,_2001_Census_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ethnic_composition_of_the_population_of_Ukraine,_2001_Census-2">[2]</a></sup> It was also estimated that there were about 10–40,000 <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">Koreans</a> in Ukraine, who lived mostly in the south of the country, belonging to the historical <a href="/wiki/Koryo-saram" title="Koryo-saram">Koryo-saram</a> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[281]</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside the former Soviet Union, the largest source of incoming immigrants in Ukraine's post-independence period was from four Asian countries, namely China, India, Pakistan and Afghanistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mp_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-mp-287">[282]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 2010s 1.4 million Ukrainians were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internally_displaced" title="Internally displaced">internally displaced</a> due to the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Donbas_(2014%E2%80%932022)" title="War in Donbas (2014–2022)">war in Donbas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[283]</a></sup> and in early 2022 over 4.1 million fled the country in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/2022_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine" title="2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine">Russian invasion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[284]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_language_in_Ukraine" title="Russian language in Ukraine">Russian language in Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Ukraine" title="Languages of Ukraine">Languages of Ukraine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Name_of_Ukraine" title="Name of Ukraine">Name of Ukraine</a></div>
<p>According to the constitution, the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">state language</a> of Ukraine is Ukrainian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SerhyYUBoaMN_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-SerhyYUBoaMN-290">[285]</a></sup> Russian is widely spoken, especially in eastern and southern Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SerhyYUBoaMN_290-1"><a href="#cite_note-SerhyYUBoaMN-290">[285]</a></sup> Most native Ukrainian speakers know Russian as a second language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SerhyYUBoaMN_290-2"><a href="#cite_note-SerhyYUBoaMN-290">[285]</a></sup> Russian was the <i>de facto</i> dominant language of the Soviet Union but Ukrainian also held official status<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[286]</a></sup> and in the schools of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ukrainian_SSR" title="Ukrainian SSR">Ukrainian SSR</a> learning Ukrainian was mandatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SerhyYUBoaMN_290-3"><a href="#cite_note-SerhyYUBoaMN-290">[285]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1720" data-file-width="2500" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png/290px-UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png/435px-UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png/580px-UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UkraineNativeLanguagesCensus2001detailed-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Linguistic map of Ukraine showing most common native language by city, town or village council according to the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Census_(2001)" title="Ukrainian Census (2001)">2001 census</a></div></div></div>
<p>Effective in August 2012, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislation_on_languages_in_Ukraine" title="Legislation on languages in Ukraine">a new law on regional languages</a> entitled any local language spoken by at least a 10 percent minority be declared official within that area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[287]</a></sup> Within weeks, Russian was declared a regional language of several southern and eastern <a href="/wiki/Oblasts_of_Ukraine" title="Oblasts of Ukraine">oblasts</a> (provinces) and cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[288]</a></sup> Russian could then be used in the administrative office work and documents of those places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewUklang2892012_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewUklang2892012-294">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[290]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 February 2014, following the <a href="/wiki/Revolution_of_Dignity" title="Revolution of Dignity">Revolution of Dignity</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Parliament" title="Ukrainian Parliament">Ukrainian Parliament</a> voted to repeal the law on regional languages, making Ukrainian the sole state language at all levels; however, the repeal was not signed by acting <a href="/wiki/Oleksandr_Turchynov" title="Oleksandr Turchynov">President Turchynov</a> or by President Poroshenko.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[291]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[293]</a></sup> In February 2019, the law allowing for official use of regional languages was found unconstitutional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[294]</a></sup> According to the Council of Europe, this act fails to achieve fair protection of the <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_rights" title="Linguistic rights">linguistic rights</a> of <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">minorities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[295]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ukrainian is the primary language used in the vast majority of Ukraine (see "Linguistic map of Ukraine" above.) 67% of Ukrainians speak Ukrainian as their primary language, while 30% speak Russian as their primary language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[296]</a></sup> In eastern and southern Ukraine, Russian is the primary language in some cities, while Ukrainian is used in rural areas. <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> is spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Zakarpattia_Oblast" title="Zakarpattia Oblast">Zakarpattia Oblast</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unian.info_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-unian.info-302">[297]</a></sup>
</p><p>For a large part of the Soviet era, the number of Ukrainian speakers declined from generation to generation, and by the mid-1980s, the usage of the Ukrainian language in public life had decreased significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shamshur_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shamshur-303">[298]</a></sup> Following independence, the government of Ukraine began restoring the use of the Ukrainian language in schools and government through a policy of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ukrainisation" title="Ukrainisation">Ukrainisation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[300]</a></sup> Today, most foreign films and TV programs, including Russian ones, are subtitled or dubbed in Ukrainian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[301]</a></sup> Ukraine's 2017 <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Ukraine" title="Education in Ukraine">education law</a> bars primary education in public schools in grade five and up in any language but Ukrainian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[303]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diaspora">Diaspora</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">These paragraphs are an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_diaspora" title="Ukrainian diaspora">Ukrainian diaspora</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ukrainian_diaspora&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
The Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a> comprises <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a> and their descendants who live outside Ukraine around the world, especially those who maintain some kind of connection, even if <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephemeral" title="Ephemeral">ephemeral</a>, to the land of their ancestors and maintain their feeling of Ukrainian national identity within their own local community. The Ukrainian diaspora is found throughout numerous regions worldwide including other <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_states" title="Post-Soviet states">post-Soviet states</a> as well as in other countries such as <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians_in_Poland" title="Ukrainians in Poland">Poland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[304]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Americans" title="Ukrainian Americans">the United States</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[305]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Canadians" title="Ukrainian Canadians">Canada</a>, the UK<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[307]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[308]</a></sup></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Ukraine" title="Religion in Ukraine">Religion in Ukraine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3603" data-file-width="4800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:80-391-0151_Kyiv_St.Sophia%27s_Cathedral_RB_18_2_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Sophia%27s_Cathedral,_Kyiv" title="Saint Sophia's Cathedral, Kyiv">Saint Sophia Cathedral in Kyiv</a>, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[309]</a></sup> is one of the main Christian cathedrals in Ukraine.</div></div></div>
<p>Ukraine has the world's <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_by_country" title="Eastern Orthodoxy by country">second-largest Eastern Orthodox population</a>, after Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[310]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[311]</a></sup> A 2021 survey conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Kyiv_International_Institute_of_Sociology" title="Kyiv International Institute of Sociology">Kyiv International Institute of Sociology</a> (KIIS) found that 82% of Ukrainians declared themselves to be religious, while 7% were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheists" title="Atheists">atheists</a>, and a further 11% found it difficult to answer the question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[312]</a></sup> The level of religiosity in Ukraine was reported to be the highest in <a href="/wiki/Western_Ukraine" title="Western Ukraine">Western Ukraine</a> (91%), and the lowest in the <a href="/wiki/Donbas" title="Donbas">Donbas</a> (57%) and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Ukraine" title="Eastern Ukraine">Eastern Ukraine</a> (56%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razumkov2016Page27_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-Razumkov2016Page27-318">[313]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, 82% of Ukrainians were Christians; out of which 72.7% declared themselves to be <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Orthodox</a>, 8.8% <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Greek_Catholic_Church" title="Ukrainian Greek Catholic Church">Greek Rite Catholics</a>, 2.3% <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestants</a> and 0.9% <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Rite Catholics</a>. Other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a> comprised 2.3%. <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> were the religions of 0.2% of the population each. According to the KIIS study, roughly 58.3% of the Ukrainian Orthodox population were members of the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church_of_Ukraine" title="Orthodox Church of Ukraine">Orthodox Church of Ukraine</a>, and 25.4% were members of the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Orthodox_Church_(Moscow_Patriarchate)" title="Ukrainian Orthodox Church (Moscow Patriarchate)">Ukrainian Orthodox Church (Moscow Patriarchate)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[314]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a 2018 survey by the Razumkov Centre, 9.4% of Ukrainians were <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Greek_Catholic_Church" title="Ukrainian Greek Catholic Church">Byzantine Rite Catholics</a> and 0.8% were <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Latin Rite Catholics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razumkov2016Page29_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-Razumkov2016Page29-320">[315]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants_in_Ukraine" title="Protestants in Ukraine">Protestants</a> are a growing community in Ukraine, who made up 1.9% of the population in 2016,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Razumkov2016Page29_320-1"><a href="#cite_note-Razumkov2016Page29-320">[315]</a></sup> but rose to 2.2% of the population in 2018.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Ukraine" title="Health in Ukraine">Health in Ukraine</a></div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid <p>Ukraine's healthcare system is state subsidised and freely available to all Ukrainian citizens and registered residents. However, it is not compulsory to be treated in a state-run hospital as a number of private medical complexes do exist nationwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[316]</a></sup> The public sector employs most healthcare professionals, with those working for private medical centres typically also retaining their state employment as they are mandated to provide care at public health facilities on a regular basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[317]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_(%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA)_-_04.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_%28%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA%29_-_04.JPG/200px-%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_%28%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA%29_-_04.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_%28%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA%29_-_04.JPG/300px-%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_%28%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA%29_-_04.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_%28%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA%29_-_04.JPG/400px-%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_%28%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA%29_-_04.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D1%96%D1%81%D1%8C%D0%BA%D0%B0_%D0%B4%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%8F%D1%87%D0%B0_%D0%BB%D1%96%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%BD%D1%8F_(%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%87%D1%83%D0%BA)_-_04.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The municipal children's hospital in <a href="/wiki/Kremenchuk" title="Kremenchuk">Kremenchuk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poltava_Oblast" title="Poltava Oblast">Poltava Oblast</a></div></div></div>
<p>All of Ukraine's medical service providers and hospitals are subordinate to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Healthcare_(Ukraine)" title="Ministry of Healthcare (Ukraine)">Ministry of Healthcare</a>, which provides oversight and scrutiny of general medical practice as well as being responsible for the day-to-day administration of the healthcare system. Despite this, standards of hygiene and patient-care have fallen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[318]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ukraine faces a number of major public health issues and is considered to be in a demographic crisis because of its high death rate and low birth rate (the Ukrainian birth rate is 11 births/1,000 population, and the death rate is 16.3 deaths/1,000 population).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A factor contributing to the high death rate is a high <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a> among working-age males from preventable causes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_poisoning" title="Alcohol poisoning">alcohol poisoning</a> and smoking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldbank1_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank1-324">[319]</a></sup> In addition, obesity, systemic high blood pressure and the HIV endemic are all major challenges facing the Ukrainian healthcare system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Active reformation of Ukraine's healthcare system was initiated right after the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Ulana_Suprun" title="Ulana Suprun">Ulana Suprun</a> as a head of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Healthcare_(Ukraine)" title="Ministry of Healthcare (Ukraine)">Ministry of Healthcare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rada_Reform_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rada_Reform-325">[320]</a></sup> Assisted by deputy Pavlo Kovtoniuk, Suprun first changed the distribution of finances in healthcare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kovtoniuk_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kovtoniuk-326">[321]</a></sup> Funds must follow the patient. General practitioners will provide basic care for patients. The patient will have the right to choose one. Emergency medical service is considered to be fully funded by the state. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_Medicine_Reform_in_Ukraine_since_2016" title="Emergency Medicine Reform in Ukraine since 2016">Emergency Medicine Reform</a> is also an important part of the healthcare reform. In addition, patients who suffer from chronic diseases, which cause a high toll of disability and mortality, are provided with free or low-price medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drugs_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drugs-327">[322]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Ukraine" title="Education in Ukraine">Education in Ukraine</a></div>

//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/%D0%A0%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%B4%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%86%D1%96%D1%8F_%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%96%D0%B2_%D0%91%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D1%96_%D0%94%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%86%D1%96%D1%97_5.jpg/400px-%D0%A0%D0%B5%D0%B7%D0%B8%D0%B4%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%86%D1%96%D1%8F_%D0%BC%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%80%D0%BE%D0%BF%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B8%D1%82%D1%96%D0%B2_%D0%91%D1%83%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%B8_%D1%96_%D0%94%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BC%D0%B0%D1%86%D1%96%D1%97_5.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The <a href="/wiki/Residence_of_Bukovinian_and_Dalmatian_Metropolitans" title="Residence of Bukovinian and Dalmatian Metropolitans">Residence of Bukovinian and Dalmatian Metropolitans</a> by <a href="/wiki/Josef_Hl%C3%A1vka" title="Josef Hlávka">Josef Hlávka</a>, 1882, now <a href="/wiki/Chernivtsi_University" title="Chernivtsi University">Chernivtsi University</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ukraine" title="Constitution of Ukraine">Ukrainian constitution</a>, access to free education is granted to all citizens. Complete general secondary education is compulsory in the state schools which constitute the overwhelming majority. Free higher education in state and communal educational establishments is provided on a competitive basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[323]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the Soviet Union's emphasis on total access of education for all citizens, which continues today, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literacy_rate" title="Literacy rate">literacy rate</a> is an estimated 99.4%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-cia-55">[50]</a></sup> Since 2005, an eleven-year school programme has been replaced with a twelve-year one: primary education takes four years to complete (starting at age six), middle education (secondary) takes five years to complete; upper secondary then takes three years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[324]</a></sup> Students in the 12th grade take Government tests, which are also referred to as school-leaving exams. These tests are later used for university admissions.
</p><p>Among the oldest is also the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lviv_University" title="Lviv University">Lviv University</a>, founded in 1661. More higher education institutions were set up in the 19th century, beginning with universities in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kharkiv_University" title="Kharkiv University">Kharkiv</a> (1805), <a href="/wiki/Taras_Shevchenko_National_University_of_Kyiv" title="Taras Shevchenko National University of Kyiv">Kyiv</a> (1834), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odessa_University" title="Odessa University">Odesa</a> (1865) and <a href="/wiki/Chernivtsi_University" title="Chernivtsi University">Chernivtsi</a> (1875) and a number of professional higher education institutions, e.g.: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nizhyn_Pedagogical_University" title="Nizhyn Pedagogical University">Nizhyn Historical and Philological Institute</a> (originally established as the Gymnasium of Higher Sciences in 1805), a Veterinary Institute (1873) and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kharkiv_Polytechnical_Institute" title="Kharkiv Polytechnical Institute">Technological Institute</a> (1885) in <a href="/wiki/Kharkiv" title="Kharkiv">Kharkiv</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Igor_Sikorsky_Kyiv_Polytechnic_Institute" title="Igor Sikorsky Kyiv Polytechnic Institute">Polytechnic Institute</a> in Kyiv (1898) and a Higher Mining School (1899) in <a href="/wiki/Dnipro" title="Dnipro">Katerynoslav</a>. Rapid growth followed in the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Ukrainian Soviet Socialist Republic">Soviet</a> period. By 1988 the number of higher education institutions increased to 146 with over 850,000 students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[325]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Ukrainian higher education system comprises higher educational establishments, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific" title="Scientific">scientific</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodological" title="Methodological">methodological</a> facilities under national, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Municipal_government" title="Municipal government">municipal</a> and self-governing bodies in charge of education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[326]</a></sup> The organisation of higher education in Ukraine is built up in accordance with the structure of education of the world's higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_countries" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a>, as is defined by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> and the UN.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[327]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ukraine produces the fourth largest number of <a href="/wiki/Tertiary_education" title="Tertiary education">post-secondary graduates</a> in Europe, while being ranked seventh in population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[328]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Ukraine" title="Higher education in Ukraine">Higher education</a> is either state funded or private. Most universities provide subsidised housing for out-of-city students. It is common for libraries to supply required books for all registered students. Ukrainian universities confer two degrees: the bachelor's degree (4 years) and the master's degree (5–6th year), in accordance with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bologna_process" title="Bologna process">Bologna process</a>. Historically, <a href="/wiki/Specialist_degree" title="Specialist degree">Specialist degree</a> (usually 5 years) is still also granted; it was the only degree awarded by universities in Soviet times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[329]</a></sup> Ukraine was ranked 49th in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Innovation_Index" title="Global Innovation Index">Global Innovation Index</a> in 2021<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[330]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_differences">Regional differences</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Ukraine#Regional_differences" title="Demographics of Ukraine">Demographics of Ukraine § Regional differences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Ukraine" title="Central Ukraine">Central Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Ukraine" title="Eastern Ukraine">Eastern Ukraine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ukraine" title="Southern Ukraine">Southern Ukraine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Western_Ukraine" title="Western Ukraine">Western Ukraine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="817" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png/290px-Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png/435px-Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png/580px-Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ukr_elections_2014_multimandate_okruhs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The results of the <a href="/wiki/2014_Ukrainian_parliamentary_election" title="2014 Ukrainian parliamentary election">2014 parliamentary election</a> with <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Front_(Ukraine)" title="People's Front (Ukraine)">People's Front</a> in yellow, <a href="/wiki/Opposition_Bloc" title="Opposition Bloc">Opposition Bloc</a> in blue and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petro_Poroshenko_Bloc" title="Petro Poroshenko Bloc">Petro Poroshenko Bloc</a> in red</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a> is the dominant language in <a href="/wiki/Western_Ukraine" title="Western Ukraine">Western Ukraine</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Central_Ukraine" title="Central Ukraine">Central Ukraine</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> is the dominant language in the cities of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Ukraine" title="Eastern Ukraine">Eastern Ukraine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ukraine" title="Southern Ukraine">Southern Ukraine</a>. In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ukrainian_SSR" title="Ukrainian SSR">Ukrainian SSR</a> schools, learning <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> was mandatory; in modern Ukraine, schools with Ukrainian as the language of instruction offer classes in Russian and in the other minority languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SerhyYUBoaMN_290-4"><a href="#cite_note-SerhyYUBoaMN-290">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[331]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RatingJuly12_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-RatingJuly12-337">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[333]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the <a href="/wiki/Russian_language_in_Ukraine" title="Russian language in Ukraine">Russian language</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_nationalism" title="Ukrainian nationalism">Ukrainian nationalism</a>, opinion in Eastern Ukraine and Southern Ukraine tends to be the exact opposite of those in Western Ukraine; while opinions in Central Ukraine on these topics tend be less extreme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RatingJuly12_337-1"><a href="#cite_note-RatingJuly12-337">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KIISS1313_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-KIISS1313-341">[336]</a></sup>
</p><p>Similar historical cleavages also remain evident at the level of individual social identification. Attitudes toward the most important political issue, relations with Russia, differed strongly between <a href="/wiki/Lviv" title="Lviv">Lviv</a>, identifying more with <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_nationalism" title="Ukrainian nationalism">Ukrainian nationalism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Greek_Catholic_Church" title="Ukrainian Greek Catholic Church">Ukrainian Greek Catholic Church</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Donetsk" title="Donetsk">Donetsk</a>, predominantly Russian orientated and favourable to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_era" title="Soviet era">Soviet era</a>, while in central and southern Ukraine, as well as <a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kyiv</a>, such divisions were less important and there was less antipathy toward people from other regions (a poll by the <a href="/wiki/Research_%26_Branding_Group" title="Research &amp; Branding Group">Research &amp; Branding Group</a> held March 2010 showed that the attitude of the citizens of Donetsk to the citizens of Lviv was 79% positive and that the attitude of the citizens of Lviv to the citizens of Donetsk was 88% positive).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-antipathy_342-0"><a href="#cite_note-antipathy-342">[337]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, all were united by an overarching Ukrainian identity based on shared economic difficulties, showing that other attitudes are determined more by culture and politics than by demographic differences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-antipathy_342-1"><a href="#cite_note-antipathy-342">[337]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[338]</a></sup> Surveys of regional identities in Ukraine have shown that the feeling of belonging to a "Soviet identity" is strongest in the <a href="/wiki/Donbas" title="Donbas">Donbas</a> (about 40%) and the Crimea (about 30%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[339]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Ukraine" title="Elections in Ukraine">elections</a> voters of Western and Central Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Oblasts_of_Ukraine" title="Oblasts of Ukraine">oblasts</a> (provinces) vote mostly for parties (<a href="/wiki/Our_Ukraine%E2%80%93People%27s_Self-Defense_Bloc" title="Our Ukraine–People's Self-Defense Bloc">Our Ukraine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-Ukrainian_Union_%22Fatherland%22" title='All-Ukrainian Union "Fatherland"'>Batkivshchyna</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[341]</a></sup> and presidential candidates (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viktor_Yuschenko" title="Viktor Yuschenko">Viktor Yuschenko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yulia_Tymoshenko" title="Yulia Tymoshenko">Yulia Tymoshenko</a>) with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pro-Western" title="Pro-Western">pro-Western</a> and state reform <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_platform" title="Political platform">platform</a>, while voters in Southern and Eastern oblasts vote for parties (<a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Ukraine" title="Communist Party of Ukraine">CPU</a>, <a href="/wiki/Party_of_Regions" title="Party of Regions">Party of Regions</a>) and presidential candidates (<a href="/wiki/Viktor_Yanukovych" title="Viktor Yanukovych">Viktor Yanukovych</a>) with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pro-Russian" title="Pro-Russian">pro-Russian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Status_quo" title="Status quo">status quo</a> platform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWparties_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWparties-347">[342]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umland_348-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umland-348">[343]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[344]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[345]</a></sup> However, this geographical division is decreasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[347]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[348]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ukrainian_culture" title="Ukrainian culture">Ukrainian culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pysanky2011.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2224" data-file-width="3250" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Pysanky2011.JPG/220px-Pysanky2011.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Pysanky2011.JPG/330px-Pysanky2011.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Pysanky2011.JPG/440px-Pysanky2011.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pysanky2011.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A collection of traditional Ukrainian Easter eggs—<a href="/wiki/Egg_decorating_in_Slavic_culture" title="Egg decorating in Slavic culture">pysanky</a>. The design motifs on pysanky date back to early Slavic cultures.</div></div></div>
<p>Ukrainian customs are heavily influenced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Christianity" title="Orthodox Christianity">Orthodox Christianity</a>, the dominant religion in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[349]</a></sup> Gender roles also tend to be more traditional, and grandparents play a greater role in bringing up children, than in the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[350]</a></sup> The culture of Ukraine has also been influenced by its eastern and western neighbours, reflected in its <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_architecture" title="Ukrainian architecture">architecture</a>, music and art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-356"><a href="#cite_note-356">[351]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Communist era had quite a strong effect on the art and writing of Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[352]</a></sup> In 1932, Stalin made <a href="/wiki/Socialist_realism" title="Socialist realism">socialist realism</a> state policy in the Soviet Union when he promulgated the decree "On the Reconstruction of Literary and Art Organisations". This greatly stifled creativity. During the 1980s <a href="/wiki/Glasnost" title="Glasnost">glasnost</a> (openness) was introduced and Soviet artists and writers again became free to express themselves as they wanted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[353]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ukraine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> inscribed <a href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Ukraine" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Ukraine">seven properties in Ukraine on the World Heritage List</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[354]</a></sup> Ukraine is also known for its decorative and folk traditions such as <a href="/wiki/Petrykivka_painting" title="Petrykivka painting">Petrykivka painting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosiv" title="Kosiv">Kosiv</a> ceramics, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cossack%27s_songs_of_Dnipropetrovsk_Region&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cossack's songs of Dnipropetrovsk Region (page does not exist)">Cossack songs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[355]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[356]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[357]</a></sup>
</p><p>The tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Easter_egg" title="Easter egg">Easter egg</a>, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pysanky" title="Pysanky">pysanky</a>, has long roots in Ukraine. These eggs were drawn on with wax to create a pattern; then, the dye was applied to give the eggs their pleasant colours, the dye did not affect the previously wax-coated parts of the egg. After the entire egg was dyed, the wax was removed leaving only the colourful pattern. This tradition is thousands of years old, and precedes the arrival of Christianity to Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-363"><a href="#cite_note-363">[358]</a></sup> In the city of <a href="/wiki/Kolomyia" title="Kolomyia">Kolomyia</a> near the foothills of the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a>, the museum of Pysanka was built in 2000 and won a nomination as the monument of modern Ukraine in 2007, part of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_Ukraine" title="Seven Wonders of Ukraine">Seven Wonders of Ukraine</a> action.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_literature" title="Ukrainian literature">Ukrainian literature</a></div>
<p>Technically the history of Ukrainian literature dates all of the way back to the 11th century, following the Christianisation of Kievan Rus', however these earliest writings were <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgical</a> and were written in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Church Slavonic</a> language, not in true Ukrainian. Historical accounts of the time were referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">chronicles</a></i>, the most significant of which was the <a href="/wiki/Primary_Chronicle" title="Primary Chronicle">Primary Chronicle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualit_364-0"><a href="#cite_note-ualit-364">[359]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualitmsn_365-0"><a href="#cite_note-ualitmsn-365">[360]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_Gnone"><a href="#endnote_Gnone">[g]</a></sup> Literary activity faced a sudden decline during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus%27" title="Mongol invasion of Rus'">Mongol invasion of Rus'</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualit_364-1"><a href="#cite_note-ualit-364">[359]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Lesya_Ukrainka_portrait.jpg/274px-Lesya_Ukrainka_portrait.jpg 2x" width="137"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Lesya_Ukrainka" title="Lesya Ukrainka">Lesya Ukrainka</a>, one of the foremost Ukrainian women writers</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Ukrainian literature again began to develop in the 14th century, and was advanced significantly in the 16th century with the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> and with the beginning of the Cossack era, under both Russian and Polish dominance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualit_364-2"><a href="#cite_note-ualit-364">[359]</a></sup> The Cossacks established an independent society and popularized a <a href="/wiki/Duma_(epic)" title="Duma (epic)">new kind</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epic_poem" title="Epic poem">epic poem</a>, which marked a high point of Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Oral_literature" title="Oral literature">oral literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualitmsn_365-1"><a href="#cite_note-ualitmsn-365">[360]</a></sup> These advances were then set back in the 17th and early 18th centuries, when publishing in the Ukrainian language was outlawed. Nonetheless, by the late 18th century modern literary Ukrainian finally emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualit_364-3"><a href="#cite_note-ualit-364">[359]</a></sup> In 1798 the modern era of the Ukrainian literary tradition began with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ivan_Kotlyarevsky" title="Ivan Kotlyarevsky">Ivan Kotlyarevsky</a>'s publication of <a href="/wiki/Eneida" title="Eneida">Eneida</a> in the Ukrainian vernacular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-366"><a href="#cite_note-366">[361]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 1830s, a Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">romantic literature</a> began to develop, and the nation's most renowned cultural figure, romanticist poet-painter <a href="/wiki/Taras_Shevchenko" title="Taras Shevchenko">Taras Shevchenko</a> emerged. Whereas Ivan Kotliarevsky is considered to be the father of literature in the Ukrainian vernacular; Shevchenko is the father of a national revival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[362]</a></sup>
</p><p>Then, in 1863, the use of the Ukrainian language in print was effectively <a href="/wiki/Ems_Ukaz" title="Ems Ukaz">prohibited</a> by the Russian Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-censor_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-censor-77">[72]</a></sup> This severely curtailed literary activity in the area, and Ukrainian writers were forced to either publish their works in Russian or release them in Austrian controlled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galicia_(Central_Europe)" title="Galicia (Central Europe)">Galicia</a>. The ban was never officially lifted, but it became obsolete after the revolution and the Bolsheviks' coming to power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualitmsn_365-2"><a href="#cite_note-ualitmsn-365">[360]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ukrainian literature continued to flourish in the early Soviet years when nearly all literary trends were approved. These policies faced a steep decline in the 1930s, when prominent representatives as well as many others were killed by the <a href="/wiki/NKVD" title="NKVD">NKVD</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Purge" title="Great Purge">Great Purge</a>. In general around 223 writers were repressed by what was known as the <a href="/wiki/Executed_Renaissance" title="Executed Renaissance">Executed Renaissance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[363]</a></sup> These repressions were part of Stalin's implemented policy of <a href="/wiki/Socialist_realism" title="Socialist realism">socialist realism</a>. The doctrine did not necessarily repress the use of the Ukrainian language, but it required that writers follow a certain style in their works.
</p><p>Literary freedom grew in the late 1980s and early 1990s alongside the decline and collapse of the USSR and the reestablishment of Ukrainian independence in 1991.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ualit_364-4"><a href="#cite_note-ualit-364">[359]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_architecture" title="Ukrainian architecture">Ukrainian architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3600" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg/220px-80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg/330px-80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg/440px-80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:80-391-9007_Kyiv_St.Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Monastery_RB_18.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Golden-Domed_Cathedral" title="St. Michael's Golden-Domed Cathedral">St. Michael's Golden-Domed Cathedral</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kyiv</a>, foremost example of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cossack_Baroque" title="Cossack Baroque">Cossack Baroque</a> and one of Ukraine's most recognizable landmarks</div></div></div>
<p>Ukrainian architecture includes the motifs and styles that are found in structures built in modern Ukraine, and by <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a> worldwide.
These include initial roots which were established in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Slavs" title="Eastern Slavs">Eastern Slavic</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Kievan_Rus%27" title="Christianization of Kievan Rus'">Christianization of Kievan Rus'</a> for several ages Ukrainian architecture was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_architecture" title="Byzantine architecture">Byzantine architecture</a>. After the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus" title="Mongol invasion of Rus">12th century</a>, the distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architectural_history" title="Architectural history">architectural history</a> continued in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Galicia-Volhynia" title="Kingdom of Galicia-Volhynia">Kingdom of Galicia-Volhynia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KatchanovskiKohutNebesio2013_369-0"><a href="#cite_note-KatchanovskiKohutNebesio2013-369">[364]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the union with the <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Tsardom of Russia</a>, architecture in Ukraine began to develop in different directions, with many structures in the larger eastern, Russian-ruled area built in the styles of <a href="/wiki/Russian_architecture" title="Russian architecture">Russian architecture</a> of that period, whilst the western region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galicia_(Central_Europe)" title="Galicia (Central Europe)">Galicia</a> developed under <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Poland" title="Architecture of Poland">Polish</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Austria" title="Architecture of Austria">Austro-Hungarian architectural influences</a>. Ukrainian national motifs would eventually be used during the period of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and in modern independent Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KatchanovskiKohutNebesio2013_369-1"><a href="#cite_note-KatchanovskiKohutNebesio2013-369">[364]</a></sup> However, much of the contemporary architectural skyline of Ukraine is dominated by Soviet-style <a href="/wiki/Khrushchyovka" title="Khrushchyovka">Khrushchyovkas</a>, or low-cost apartment buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[365]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Weaving_and_embroidery">Weaving and embroidery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="848" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg/220px-Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg/330px-Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg/440px-Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rushnyk_Ukraine_embroidered_decorative_towels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rushnyk" title="Rushnyk">Rushnyk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_embroidery" title="Ukrainian embroidery">Ukrainian embroidery</a></div></div></div>
<p>Artisan <a href="/wiki/Textile_arts" title="Textile arts">textile arts</a> play an important role in Ukrainian culture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[366]</a></sup> especially in <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_wedding_traditions" title="Ukrainian wedding traditions">Ukrainian wedding traditions</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_embroidery" title="Ukrainian embroidery">Ukrainian embroidery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weaving" title="Weaving">weaving</a> and lace-making are used in traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Folk_dress" title="Folk dress">folk dress</a> and in traditional celebrations. Ukrainian embroidery varies depending on the region of origin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[367]</a></sup> and the designs have a long history of motifs, compositions, choice of colours and types of stitches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-museum_373-0"><a href="#cite_note-museum-373">[368]</a></sup> Use of colour is very important and has roots in <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_folklore" title="Ukrainian folklore">Ukrainian folklore</a>. Embroidery motifs found in different parts of Ukraine are preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Rushnyk" title="Rushnyk">Rushnyk</a> Museum in <a href="/wiki/Pereiaslav" title="Pereiaslav">Pereiaslav</a>.
</p><p>National dress is woven and highly decorated. Weaving with handmade looms is still practised in the village of Krupove, situated in <a href="/wiki/Rivne_Oblast" title="Rivne Oblast">Rivne Oblast</a>. The village is the birthplace of two famous personalities in the scene of national crafts fabrication. Nina Myhailivna<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[369]</a></sup> and Uliana Petrivna<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[370]</a></sup> with international recognition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Ukraine" title="Music of Ukraine">Music of Ukraine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_(1890).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1068" data-file-width="998" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_%281890%29.jpg/220px-Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_%281890%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_%281890%29.jpg/330px-Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_%281890%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_%281890%29.jpg/440px-Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_%281890%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fedir_Stovbynenko_-_Kozak-bandyryst_(1890).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cossack_Mamay" title="Cossack Mamay">Cossack Mamay</a> playing a <a href="/wiki/Kobza" title="Kobza">kobza</a></div></div></div>
<p>Music is a major part of Ukrainian culture, with a long history and many influences. From traditional <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk music</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">classical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_rock" title="Modern rock">modern rock</a>, Ukraine has produced several internationally recognised musicians including <a href="/wiki/Kirill_Karabits" title="Kirill Karabits">Kirill Karabits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okean_Elzy" title="Okean Elzy">Okean Elzy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruslana" title="Ruslana">Ruslana</a>. Elements from traditional Ukrainian folk music made their way into Western music and even into modern <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>. Ukrainian music sometimes presents a perplexing mix of exotic melismatic singing with chordal harmony. The most striking general characteristic of authentic ethnic Ukrainian folk music is the wide use of minor modes or keys which incorporate augmented second intervals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sonevytsky_2019_p._376-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sonevytsky_2019_p.-376">[371]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Baroque period, music had a place of considerable importance in the curriculum of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kyiv-Mohyla_Academy" title="Kyiv-Mohyla Academy">Kyiv-Mohyla Academy</a>. Much of the nobility was well versed in music with many Ukrainian Cossack leaders such as (<a href="/wiki/Ivan_Mazepa" title="Ivan Mazepa">Mazepa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semen_Paliy" title="Semen Paliy">Paliy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antin_Holovaty" title="Antin Holovaty">Holovatyj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Sirko" title="Ivan Sirko">Sirko</a>) being accomplished players of the <a href="/wiki/Kobza" title="Kobza">kobza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bandura" title="Bandura">bandura</a> or <a href="/wiki/Torban" title="Torban">torban</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9B%D0%B8%D1%81%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%BA%D0%BE_%D0%9C%D0%B8%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BB%D0%B0_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mykola_Lysenko" title="Mykola Lysenko">Mykola Lysenko</a> is widely considered to be the father of Ukrainian classical music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Risch_2011_p._44_377-0"><a href="#cite_note-Risch_2011_p._44-377">[372]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The first dedicated musical academy was set up in <a href="/wiki/Hlukhiv" title="Hlukhiv">Hlukhiv</a> in 1738 and students were taught to sing and play violin and bandura from manuscripts. As a result, many of the earliest composers and performers within the Russian empire were ethnically Ukrainian, having been born or educated in Hlukhiv or having been closely associated with this music school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struk_1993_p._1461_378-0"><a href="#cite_note-Struk_1993_p._1461-378">[373]</a></sup> Ukrainian classical music differs considerably depending on whether the composer was of Ukrainian ethnicity living in Ukraine, a composer of non-Ukrainian ethnicity who was a citizen of Ukraine, or part of the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_diaspora" title="Ukrainian diaspora">Ukrainian diaspora</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ukrainian_people_2017_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ukrainian_people_2017-379">[374]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the mid-1960s, Western-influenced pop music has been growing in popularity in Ukraine. Folk singer and harmonium player <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Sadovska" title="Mariana Sadovska">Mariana Sadovska</a> is prominent. Ukrainian pop and folk music arose with the international popularity of groups and performers like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vopli_Vidoplyasova" title="Vopli Vidoplyasova">Vopli Vidoplyasova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dakh_Daughters" title="Dakh Daughters">Dakh Daughters</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dakha_Brakha" title="Dakha Brakha">Dakha Brakha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Dorn" title="Ivan Dorn">Ivan Dorn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okean_Elzy" title="Okean Elzy">Okean Elzy</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Media_of_Ukraine" title="Media of Ukraine">Media of Ukraine</a></div>
<p>The Ukrainian legal framework on media freedom is deemed "among the most progressive in eastern Europe", although implementation has been uneven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FH_380-0"><a href="#cite_note-FH-380">[375]</a></sup> The constitution and laws provide for <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_speech" title="Freedom of speech">freedom of speech</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[376]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press_in_Ukraine" title="Freedom of the press in Ukraine">press</a>. The main regulatory authority for the broadcast media is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Television_and_Radio_Broadcasting_Council_of_Ukraine" title="National Television and Radio Broadcasting Council of Ukraine">National Television and Radio Broadcasting Council of Ukraine</a> (NTRBCU), tasked with licensing media outlets and ensure their compliance with the law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[377]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kyiv</a> dominates the media sector in Ukraine: National <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_Ukraine" title="List of newspapers in Ukraine">newspapers</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Den_(newspaper)" title="Den (newspaper)">Den</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dzerkalo_Tyzhnia" title="Dzerkalo Tyzhnia">Dzerkalo Tyzhnia</a></i>, tabloids, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ukrainian_Week" title="The Ukrainian Week">The Ukrainian Week</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Focus_(Ukrainian_magazine)" title="Focus (Ukrainian magazine)">Focus</a></i>, and television and radio are largely based there,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> although <a href="/wiki/Lviv" title="Lviv">Lviv</a> is also a significant national media centre. The National News Agency of Ukraine, <a href="/wiki/Ukrinform" title="Ukrinform">Ukrinform</a> was founded here in 1918. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BBC_Ukrainian" title="BBC Ukrainian">BBC Ukrainian</a> started its broadcasts in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[378]</a></sup> As of 2022<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ukraine&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> 75% of the population use the internet, and social media is widely used by government and people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[379]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Ukraine" title="Sport in Ukraine">Sport in Ukraine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andriy_Shevchenko_2017.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="398" decoding="async" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Andriy_Shevchenko_2017.jpg/220px-Andriy_Shevchenko_2017.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Andriy_Shevchenko_2017.jpg/330px-Andriy_Shevchenko_2017.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Andriy_Shevchenko_2017.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Andriy_Shevchenko_2017.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andriy_Shevchenko" title="Andriy Shevchenko">Andriy Shevchenko</a>, current Ukrainian football manager and former professional footballer</div></div></div>
<p>Ukraine greatly benefited from the Soviet emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Physical_education" title="Physical education">physical education</a>. These policies left Ukraine with hundreds of stadia, swimming pools, gymnasia and many other athletic facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[380]</a></sup> The most popular sport is <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>. The top professional league is the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Premier_League" title="Ukrainian Premier League">Vyscha Liha</a> ("premier league").
</p><p>Many Ukrainians also played for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_national_football_team" title="Soviet national football team">Soviet national football team</a>, most notably <a href="/wiki/Ballon_d%27Or" title="Ballon d'Or">Ballon d'Or</a> winners <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ihor_Belanov" title="Ihor Belanov">Ihor Belanov</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oleh_Blokhin" title="Oleh Blokhin">Oleh Blokhin</a>. This award was only presented to one Ukrainian after the dissolution of the Soviet Union, <a href="/wiki/Andriy_Shevchenko" title="Andriy Shevchenko">Andriy Shevchenko</a>. The national team made its debut in the <a href="/wiki/2006_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2006 FIFA World Cup">2006 FIFA World Cup</a>, and reached the quarterfinals before losing to eventual champions, <a href="/wiki/Italy_national_football_team" title="Italy national football team">Italy</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VladimirVitaliy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="597" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/VladimirVitaliy.jpg/170px-VladimirVitaliy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/VladimirVitaliy.jpg/255px-VladimirVitaliy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/VladimirVitaliy.jpg/340px-VladimirVitaliy.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VladimirVitaliy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Vitali_Klitschko" title="Vitali Klitschko">Vitali Klitschko</a> and his brother, <a href="/wiki/Wladimir_Klitschko" title="Wladimir Klitschko">Wladimir</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxers</a> are amongst the best in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-386"><a href="#cite_note-386">[381]</a></sup> Since becoming the undisputed cruiserweight champion in 2018, <a href="/wiki/Oleksandr_Usyk" title="Oleksandr Usyk">Oleksandr Usyk</a> has also gone on to win the unified WBA (Super), IBF, WBO and IBO heavyweight titles. This feat made him one of only three boxers to have unified the cruiserweight world titles and become a world heavyweight champion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[382]</a></sup> The brothers <a href="/wiki/Vitali_Klitschko" title="Vitali Klitschko">Vitali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wladimir_Klitschko" title="Wladimir Klitschko">Wladimir Klitschko</a> are former <a href="/wiki/Heavyweight" title="Heavyweight">heavyweight</a> world champions who held multiple world titles throughout their careers. Also hailing from Ukraine is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasyl_Lomachenko" title="Vasyl Lomachenko">Vasyl Lomachenko</a>, a <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2012_Olympic_games" title="2012 Olympic games">2012 Olympic</a> gold medalist. He is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unified_champion" title="Unified champion">unified</a> <a href="/wiki/Lightweight" title="Lightweight">lightweight</a> world champion who ties the record for winning a world title in the fewest professional fights; three. As of September 2018, he is ranked as the world's best active boxer, <a href="/wiki/Pound_for_pound" title="Pound for pound">pound for pound</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Boxing_pound_for_pound_rankings#ESPN" title="Boxing pound for pound rankings">ESPN</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[383]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sergey_Bubka" title="Sergey Bubka">Sergey Bubka</a> held the record in the <a href="/wiki/Pole_vault" title="Pole vault">Pole vault</a> from 1993 to 2014; with great strength, speed and gymnastic abilities, he was voted the world's best athlete on several occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[384]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-390"><a href="#cite_note-390">[385]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">Basketball</a> has gained popularity in Ukraine. In 2011, Ukraine was granted a right to organize <a href="/wiki/EuroBasket_2015" title="EuroBasket 2015">EuroBasket 2015</a>. Two years later the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ukraine_national_basketball_team" title="Ukraine national basketball team">Ukraine national basketball team</a> finished sixth in <a href="/wiki/EuroBasket_2013" title="EuroBasket 2013">EuroBasket 2013</a> and qualified to <a href="/wiki/2014_FIBA_Basketball_World_Cup" title="2014 FIBA Basketball World Cup">FIBA World Cup</a> for the first time in its history. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euroleague" title="Euroleague">Euroleague</a> participant <a href="/wiki/BC_Budivelnyk" title="BC Budivelnyk">Budivelnyk Kyiv</a> is the strongest professional basketball club in Ukraine.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">Chess</a> is a popular sport in Ukraine. <a href="/wiki/Ruslan_Ponomariov" title="Ruslan Ponomariov">Ruslan Ponomariov</a> is the former world champion. There are about 85 <a href="/wiki/Grandmaster_(chess)" title="Grandmaster (chess)">Grandmasters</a> and 198 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Masters" title="International Masters">International Masters</a> in Ukraine.<a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">Rugby league</a> is played throughout Ukraine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-391"><a href="#cite_note-391">[386]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_cuisine" title="Ukrainian cuisine">Ukrainian cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wareniki.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1872" data-file-width="3328" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Wareniki.JPG/220px-Wareniki.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Wareniki.JPG/330px-Wareniki.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Wareniki.JPG/440px-Wareniki.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wareniki.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varenyky" title="Varenyky">Varenyky</a> topped with fried onion</div></div></div>
<p>The traditional Ukrainian diet includes chicken, pork, beef, fish and mushrooms. Ukrainians also tend to eat a lot of potatoes; grains; and fresh, boiled or pickled vegetables. Popular traditional dishes <i><span title="Ukrainian-language text"><i lang="uk-Latn"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varenyky" title="Varenyky">varenyky</a></i></span></i> (boiled dumplings with mushrooms, potatoes, sauerkraut, cottage cheese, cherries or berries), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nalysnyky" title="Nalysnyky">nalysnyky</a></i> (pancakes with cottage cheese, poppy seeds, mushrooms, caviar or meat), <i><a href="/wiki/Cabbage_soup" title="Cabbage soup">kapusnyak</a></i> (cabbage soup made with meat, potatoes, carrots, onions, millet, tomato paste, spices and fresh herbs), <a href="/wiki/Borscht" title="Borscht">borscht</a> (soup made of beets, cabbage and mushrooms or meat) and <span title="Ukrainian-language text"><i lang="uk-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Go%C5%82%C4%85bki" title="Gołąbki">holubtsy</a></i></span> (stuffed cabbage rolls filled with rice, carrots, onion and minced meat).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-392"><a href="#cite_note-392">[387]</a></sup> Among traditional baked goods are decorated <a href="/wiki/Korovai" title="Korovai">korovais</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paska_(bread)" title="Paska (bread)">paska Easter bread</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-393"><a href="#cite_note-393">[388]</a></sup> Ukrainian specialties also include <a href="/wiki/Chicken_Kiev" title="Chicken Kiev">Chicken Kiev</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyiv_cake" title="Kyiv cake">Kyiv cake</a>.
</p><p>Ukrainians drink <a href="/wiki/Kompot" title="Kompot">stewed fruit compote</a>, juices, milk, <a href="/wiki/Buttermilk" title="Buttermilk">buttermilk</a>, mineral water, tea and coffee, beer, wine and <span title="Ukrainian-language text"><i lang="uk-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Horilka" title="Horilka">horilka</a></i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-394"><a href="#cite_note-394">[389]</a></sup>
</p>
